Double Liability when Master Frees a Slave with Crime and Debt
Ruling with proof on master's double liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of double liability on a master who frees a ma'dhun slave with jinayat and debt, and explain the reasoning based on the possibility of sequential satisfaction of both rights.

Core Idea
Freeing a slave with multiple claims destroys multiple rights, so the master compensates each fully.

This lesson examines a classic case from al-Hidayah: a master frees his ma'dhun slave who has committed a crime and owes debt. The master becomes liable for both claims in full. We will explore the reasoning behind this double liability and contrast it with the case where an outsider destroys the slave.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and ruling
Identify the facts: a master frees his ma'dhun slave who has both a jinayat (crime) and debt. The ruling: double liability on the master – one for the crime victim's heirs, one for the creditors.
2
Identify the two rights
The crime victim's heirs have the right to demand the slave for retaliation. The creditors have the right to sell the slave to recover the debt. Each right is fully secured against the slave.
3
Understand the proof via sequential satisfaction
In singularity (only one right), that right is fully satisfied: hand over the slave or sell him. In conjunction, it is possible to satisfy both sequentially: first hand over to heirs, then sell back to creditors. The master's freeing destroys this possibility.
The critical point: both rights could have been fully realized from the same slave.
4
Apply the reasoning to liability
Since the master's act of freeing prevented the satisfaction of both rights, he must compensate each fully. The compensation is the value of the slave (or arsh) for the crime, and the debt for the creditors, with each limited to the lesser of price or claim.
5
Contrast with the ajnabi case
If an outsider destroys the slave, only one price is due to the master (based on ownership). The master then pays that to the creditors. The outsider's liability is to the owner, not directly to the claimants, so ownership trumps the inferior rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جنایت کے لئے اس لئے کہ آقا نے ایسے دو حق تلف کئے ہیں جن میں سے ہر ایک الگ الگ پوری قیمت کے ساتھ مضمون ہوتا ہے یعنی اولیاء جنایت کے لئے غلام دینا اور غرماء کے لئے بیچنا تو ایسے ہی اجتماع کے وقت ہوگا اور ممکن ہے رقبہ واحدہ سے دونوں حقوں کا اجتماع اس طریقہ پر کہ غلام ولی جنایت کو دیدیا جائے پھر غر ماء کے لئے بیچ دیا جائے تو آقا ان دونوں کا ضامن ہوگا اتلاف کی وجہ سے۔ تشریح خالد کے غلام مازون نے کوئی جنایت کی جس پر ہزار روپے قرض ہیں اور خالد کو بھی اس کی جنایت کا علم نہیں ہوا تھا کہ خالد نے اس کو آزاد کر دیا تو اب آقا پر ڈبل ضمان ہو گا ایک ولی جنایت کے لئے اور ایک صاحب دین کے لئے قیمت اور قرض میں سے جو مقدار کم ہو وہ تو صاحب دین کے لئے واجب ہوگی اور قیمت اور ارش میں سے جو مقدار کم ہو وہ وہی جنایت کے لئے واجب ہوگی۔ کیونکہ اگر ان دونوں حقوں میں سے صرف ایک حق ہوتا تو آقا پر ایک منمان ہوتا تو جب دو حقوں کا اجتماع ہو گیا تو اب دو حقوں کا ضمان واجب ہوگا ، کیونکہ انفراد کی صورت میں غلام ولی جنایت کے حوالہ کیا جاتا اور قرض کے اندر اس کو فروخت کیا جاتا۔ سوال انفراداپور اضمان واجب ہونے سے یہ کہاں لازم آتا ہے کہ اجتماعا بھی پور اضمان واجب ہو جائے۔ جواب یہاں اس کا امکان ہے کہ ایک ہی غلام سے دونوں حق پورے پورے وصول ہو جائیں، جس کی ترکیب یہ ہے کہ غلام ولی جنایت کے حوالہ کر دیا تو اس کا پورا حق ادا ہو گیا پھر غلام کو فروخت کر دیا جائے تو غرماء کا پورا حق ادا ہو گیا۔ بہر حال یہاں آقا ان دونوں حقوق کو ضائع کرنے والا ہوا، جس کی وجہ سے اس پر ضمان واجب ہوگا۔ جس کی تفصیل گذر چکی ہے۔ آقا غلام مجرم کو آزاد کر دے آقا پر دو گنا ضمان ہے بخلاف ما إذا أتلفه أجنبى حيث تجب قيمة واحدة للمولى ويدفعها المولى إلى الغرماء لأن الأجنبي إنما يضمن للمولى بحكم الملك فلايظهر فى مقابلته الحق لأنه دونه وههنا يجب لكل واحد منهما يا تلاف الحق فلاترجيح فيظهران فيضمنهما ترجمہ بخلاف اس صورت کے جب کہ غلام کو کسی اجنبی نے تلف کیا اس حیثیت سے کہ آقا کے لئے ایک قیمت واجب ہوگی اور اس قیمت کو آقاخر ماء کو دیدے گا اس لئے کہ اجنبی آقا کے لئے ملکیت کے حکم سے ضامن ہوتا ہے پس ملک کے مقابلہ میں حق ظاہر نہ ہوگا اس لئے کہ حق ملک سے گھٹیا ہے اور یہاں ضمان واجب ہوا ہے ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کے لئے حق کے اختلاف کی وجہ سے تو کوئی ترجیح نہیں ہے پس دونوں حق ظاہر ہوں گے پس آقا ان دونوں کا ضامن ہوگا۔ تشریح صورت مذکورہ میں جب کہ آقا نے مجرم غلام کو آزاد کر دیا ہے تو آقا پر ڈبل ضمان واجب ہے۔ لیکن اگر غلام نے جنایت کی اور اس پر قرض بھی ہے اور کسی اجنبی نے اس غلام کو قتل کر دیا تو اجنبی پر صرف غلام کی قیمت ہے جو آقا کو دی جائے گی ۔ اور آقادہ قیمت قرض خواہ کو دیدے گا حالانکہ یہاں بھی اجنبی پر ڈبل منمان واجب ہونا چاہئے تھا۔ تو اس کا جواب دیا کہ یہاں اجنبی پر جو منمان واجب ہوا ہے وہ حق غریم یا حق ولی جنایت کی وجہ سے نہیں ہے بلکہ ملک آقا کی وجہ سے ہے اور ملک حق سے قوی ہوتی ہے لہذا ملک کے ساتھ حق کا ظہور نہ ہو گا۔ اس وجہ سے صرف ملک کی وجہ سے غلام کی قیمت واجب ہوگی اور آقا پر آزاد کرنے کی وجہ سے ضمان آرہا ہے وہ حقین کو اتلاف کی وجہ سے آرہا ہے جن میں ایک کو دوسرے پر ترجیح نہیں ہے لہذا جب دونوں حق برابر کے ہوئے تو آقا ان دونوں کا ضامن ہوگا۔
Because the master has caused the loss of two rights, each of which is individually secured with full value: meaning giving the slave to the heirs of the crime (for retaliation) and selling him for the creditors. So similarly at the time of conjunction it will be, and it is possible for both rights to be realized from a single entity in this manner: that the slave be handed over to the crime victim's heirs, then sold for the creditors. So the master will be guarantor for both due to causing loss. Explanation: Khalid's ma'dhun slave committed a crime for which there is a debt of a thousand rupees, and Khalid did not know about the crime when he freed him. So now double liability applies to the master: one for the crime victim's heirs, and one for the debt-holder. Whatever amount is less between the price and the debt is due to the debt-holder, and whatever is less between the price and the blood-money (arsh) is due to the crime. Because if only one of these rights existed, one liability would be on the master. When two rights combine, liability for two rights becomes due. Because in the case of singularity, the slave would be handed over to the crime victim's heirs and sold for the debt. Question: From the obligation of full liability in singularity, how does it follow that full liability is also obligatory in conjunction? Answer: Here it is possible to recover both rights fully from the same slave. The method is: if the slave is handed over to the crime victim's heirs, their full right is satisfied; then the slave is sold and the creditors' full right is satisfied. In any case, the master is the one who caused the loss of both rights, so liability is due on him. The detail has passed. If the master frees the guilty slave, he has double liability. Contrary to the case where an outsider destroys the slave: then one value is due to the master, and the master pays it to the creditors. Because the outsider is only liable to the master by virtue of ownership, so the right (of the creditors) does not appear in opposition to it, because it is inferior. Here, each of them has a right due to loss, so no preference is given; thus both rights become apparent, so the master is guarantor for both. Explanation: In the mentioned case, when the master frees the guilty slave, double liability is due on the master. But if the slave committed a crime and also had debt, and an outsider killed the slave, then only the price of the slave is due on the outsider, which is given to the master. And the master gives that price to the creditor, although here also double liability should have been due on the outsider. So the answer is given: Here the liability due on the outsider is not due to the right of the creditor or the right of the crime victim's heirs, but due to the master's ownership, and ownership is stronger than a right. Therefore, the right does not appear alongside ownership. For this reason, only the price of the slave is due due to ownership. And the liability coming on the master due to freeing is due to causing loss of both rights, in which one has no preference over the other. So when both rights are equal, the master is guarantor for both.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and comparative analysis. It addresses the scenario where a master frees his ma'dhun (permitted to trade) slave who has both committed a crime (jinayat) and owes debt. The master frees him without knowing about the crime. The ruling: the master becomes liable for both rights in full — the price for the crime victim's heirs (to compensate for the loss of the slave who would have been handed over) and the debt amount to the creditors. The reasoning is that each right individually would have been fully enforceable against the slave: the victim's heirs could demand the slave, and creditors could sell him. When both rights combine, it is possible to satisfy both in sequence: first hand over the slave to the heirs (satisfying their right fully), then sell him back to satisfy the creditors. The master, by freeing the slave, has destroyed this possibility, so he must compensate both. The passage then contrasts with the case where an ajnabi (outsider) destroys the slave. In that case, only one price is due to the master (based on ownership), and the master pays that to the creditors. The difference is that the ajnabi's liability is owed to the master qua owner, not directly to the creditors or crime victims. Ownership trumps the inferior rights of creditors, so only one value appears. But when the master himself frees, he is directly causing the loss of both specific rights, so both are equally due. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that rights arising from different causes (jinayat and debt) are independent and must be compensated fully when the master's act destroys both simultaneously.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability, obligation to compensate for loss of a right or property.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave permitted by his master to engage in trade.
جنایت (jinayat): A crime, specifically a bodily injury or homicide giving rise to diyah or qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does a master who frees his ma'dhun slave (who has committed a crime and owes debt) face double liability?
A) Because the slave had two distinct obligations, each fully satisfiable
B) Because the master is being punished for freeing without permission
C) Because the victim and creditors have equal priority
D) Because the slave's value is split equally between them

Key Takeaway
A master who frees a ma'dhun slave with both jinayat and debt guarantees both rights in full because each could have been fully satisfied from the slave sequentially.

Debt vs Jinayat: When the Child Follows the Mother
Distinction between debt and jinayat regarding child of ma'dhunah slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between debt and jinayat regarding the child of a ma'dhunah slave and apply the Hanafi principle that hukmi attributes extend (sarayan) while haqiqi acts do not.

Core Idea
Debt extends to the child; jinayat does not — hukmi attributes travel, haqiqi acts stay.

This passage from Imam Muhammad draws a sharp distinction between two obligations: debt and jinayat. Both involve a ma'dhunah slave and her child, but the child is included for debt and excluded for jinayat. The reason lies in the nature of the obligation: debt is a legal attribute (wasf hukmi) that attaches to the slave and extends to her offspring, while jinayat triggers a real act (fi'l haqiqi) of handing over, which is confined to the slave herself. Understanding this distinction is key to navigating property and liability in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two cases: (1) the ma'dhunah slave borrows more than her value then gives birth; (2) she commits a crime (jinayat) then gives birth. The ruling: in case (1) the child is sold with her; in case (2) the child is not handed over.
Focus on the different outcomes.
2
Understand the key terms
The passage introduces 'wasf hukmi' (legal attribute) and 'fi'l haqiqi' (real act). Debt is a wasf hukmi; handing over the slave for a crime is a fi'l haqiqi. Sarayan (extension) applies only to legal attributes.
These are technical Hanafi terms.
3
Trace the reasoning
Debt is a liability on the slave's own dhimma, attached to her raqabah (neck) for recovery. Being a legal attribute, it extends (yasri) to her child just as a mortgage extends to the child of a mortgaged slave. For jinayat, the obligation is on the master's liability; the master hands over the slave as a real act (physical delivery). That real act does not extend to the child.
The foundation is the difference between legal and physical.
4
Apply the mortgage analogy
The passage compares the debt case to a mortgaged slave: the child of a mortgaged slave is also covered by the mortgage. This analogy confirms that hukmi attributes extend by sarayan.
Qiyas to mortgage strengthens the rule.
5
Derive the general rule
From this specific case, abstract the principle: any hukmi attribute (like debt, mortgage) that attaches to a person/property extends to their offspring or connected property. Any haqiqi act (like physical delivery) is limited to the original subject.
This principle applies across fiqh, e.g., in waqf, dayn, and jinayat.

Source Text

قال وإذا استدانت الأمة المأذون لها أكثر من قيمتها ثم ولدت فإنه يباع الولد معها في الدين وإن جنت جناية لم يدفع الولد معها والفرق أن الدين وصف حكمى فيها واجب في ذمتها متعلق برقبتها استيفاء. فيسرى إلى الولد كولد المرهونة بخلاف الجناية لأن وجوب الدفع فى ذمة المولى لا في ذمتها وإنما يلاقيها أثر الفعل الحقيقى وهو الدفع والسراية فى الأوصاف الشرعية دون الأوصاف الحقيقية ترجمہ امام محمد نے فرمایا اور جب کہ قرض لیا اس باندی نے جس کو تجارت کی اجازت دی گئی ہے اپنی قیمت سے زیادہ پھر اس نے بچہ جنا تو قرض میں اس کے ساتھ بچہ بھی بیچا جائے گا اور اگر باندی نے جنایت کی ہو پھر اس نے بچہ جنا ہو ) تو اس کے ساتھ بچہ نہیں دیا جائے گا اور فرق یہ ہے کہ قرض باندی میں ایک حکمی وصف ہے جو باندی کے ذمہ میں واجب ہے جو باندی کی گردن سے متعلق ہے بطور وصولیابی کے تو حکمی وصف بچہ کی جانب سرایت کرے گا جیسے مرہونہ کا بچہ بخلاف جنایت کے اس لئے کہ غلام کے دینے کا وجوب ( جنایت کی وجہ سے ) آقا کے ذمہ میں ہے نہ کہ باندی کے ذمہ میں اور ملاقی ہو گیا، باندی سے فعل حقیقی کا اثر اور وہ دینا ہے اور سرایت اوصاف شرعیہ میں ہوتی ہے نہ کہ اوصاف حقیقیہ میں ۔ تشریح خالد کی ایک باندی ہے جس کو خالد نے تجارت کی اجازت دے رکھی ہے مگر اس پر قرض چڑھا ہوا ہے جو اس کی قیمت سے زیادہ ہے اور قرض کے بعد اس ماذونہ باندی کو بچہ ہوا تو اب اس قرض میں باندی کو فروخت کیا جائے گا اور اس کے ساتھ اس کے بچہ کو بھی فروخت کیا جائے گا اور اگر یہی صورت جنایت میں پیش آئی ہو کہ بعد جنایت اس نے بچہ جنا اور آقا نے یہ باندی ولی جنایت کو دینی چاہی تو اب باندی کے ساتھ باندی کا بچہ نہیں دیا جائے گا۔ بالفاظ دیگر پہلی صورت میں ماں کا حکم بچہ کی طرف سرایت کرے گا اور دوسری صورت میں ماں کا حکم بچہ کی طرف سرایت نہیں کرتا۔ اور ظاہر ہے کہ قرض ایک وصف حکمی ہے جو باندی کے ذمہ واجب ہوا ہے جس کو باندی کی گردن سے اس کو فروخت کر کے وصول کیا جاتا ہے تو یہ وصف بچہ کی طرف ایسے ہی سرایت کرے گا جیسے رہن کا حکم ولد مر ہو نہ کی طرف سرایت کرتا ہے۔ اور جنایت کی صورت میں اصل وجوب باندی نہیں بلکہ آقا پر ہے ہاں آقا اپنے اوپر وجوب کو باندی سپر د کر کے ادا کرے گا تو آقا کا اس باندی کو دینا وصف حکمی نہیں بلکہ فعل حقیقی ہے لہذا افعل حقیقی میں سرایت نہ ہو سکے گی ۔
He (Imam Muhammad) said: When a ma'dhunah female slave borrows more than her value, then gives birth, the child is sold with her for the debt. But if she commits a crime (jinayat) and then gives birth, the child is not handed over with her. The difference is that debt is a hukmi (legal) attribute in her, obligatory on her liability and attached to her neck for recovery. So it extends to the child, like the child of a mortgaged property. Contrary to crime (jinayat), because the obligation to hand over is on the master's liability, not on hers. Rather, the effect of the real (haqiqi) act — which is the handing over — meets her. And extension (sarayan) applies to legal attributes, not to real attributes. Translation: Imam Muhammad said: When a female slave who has been given permission to trade borrows more than her value, then gives birth, the child is also sold with her for the debt. But if she commits a crime and then gives birth, the child is not given with her. The difference is that debt is a legal attribute in her, obligatory on her liability and attached to her neck for recovery. So this legal attribute will extend to the child, just as the ruling of mortgage extends to the child of a mortgaged property. Contrary to crime, because the obligation of handing over (due to crime) is on the master's liability, not on hers. Rather, the effect of the real act — that is, handing over — has met her. And extension occurs in legal attributes, not in real attributes. Explanation: Khalid has a female slave whom he has permitted to trade, but she has incurred debt exceeding her value. After the debt, this ma'dhunah slave gives birth. Now, for the debt, the slave is sold, and along with her her child is also sold. If the same situation occurs in crime: after the crime she gives birth, and the master wants to hand this slave over to the crime victim's heirs, now the child is not given with the slave. In other words, in the first case, the mother's ruling extends to the child; in the second case, the mother's ruling does not extend to the child. It is obvious that the debt is a legal attribute that became obligatory on the slave's liability, recovered by selling her from her neck. So this attribute will extend to the child just as the ruling of mortgage extends to the child of a mortgaged property. In the case of crime, the original obligation is not on the slave but on the master. Yes, the master fulfills his obligation by handing over the slave. So the master's handing over of this slave is not a legal attribute but a real act. Therefore, extension does not occur in real acts.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with a comparative distinction (farq) between debt and jinayat in the context of a ma'dhunah female slave. The ruling from Imam Muhammad: When a ma'dhunah slave incurs debt exceeding her value and then gives birth, the child is sold along with her to satisfy the debt. However, if she commits a crime (jinayat) and then gives birth, the child is not handed over with her to the victim's heirs. The reasoning is based on the nature of each obligation. Debt is a hukmi (legal) attribute: it is a liability on the slave's own dhimma (legal capacity) and attaches to her neck (raqabah) for recovery. Being a legal attribute, it extends to her child, just as the right of mortgage extends to the offspring of a mortgaged slave. In contrast, jinayat creates an obligation on the master, not on the slave herself. The master must hand over the slave as a recompense, but this is a haqiqi (real) act — the physical act of giving. Real acts do not extend by sarayan (legal extension) to offspring. Thus, the child is not included. This illustrates a key Hanafi principle: hukmi attributes (like debt, mortgage) can extend to related persons/property, while haqiqi consequences (like physical delivery) are confined to the original subject.

Key Terms
وصف حكمى (wasf hukmi): A legal attribute that is established by Shariah and can extend by sarayan.
سرایت (sarayan): Legal extension of a ruling from one subject to another connected to it.
فعل حقیقی (fi'l haqiqi): A real, physical act such as handing over a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because debt is on the slave, jinayat is on the master
D) Both A and C

Key Takeaway
Debt as a legal attribute extends to the child; the handing over for jinayat as a real act does not — the key is the nature of the obligation.

Claim of Freedom Bars Diyah Claim After Slave's Killing
Ruling on liability after claiming freedom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a claimant's own assertion of a slave's freedom bars his claim for diyah after the slave kills his wali, and explain the legal reasoning based on the shifting of liability to the 'aqilah.

Core Idea
Claiming a slave is free shifts liability from slave and master to the 'aqilah, but without proof, the claimant gets nothing.

This lesson examines a concise ruling from al-Hidayah on a layered scenario involving a claim of freedom and an accidental killing. The ruling illustrates how a litigant's own legal assertion can inadvertently destroy his right to compensation. By tracing the reasoning, we see the interplay between admission, liability, and the burden of proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their relationships
There are three key persons: the master (who owns the slave), the slave, and a third claimant (who claims the master freed the slave). The slave accidentally kills the wali (guardian) of this claimant.
The wali is a close relative of the claimant.
2
Understand the claimant's assertion
The claimant states that the master had freed the slave. By this claim, he admits that the slave is now free (at least in his own view). In Hanafi fiqh, a free man's accidental killing liability falls on his 'aqilah, not on himself or his former master.
The 'aqilah are the paternal male relatives or tribal supporters.
3
Trace the legal consequence of the claim
Because the claimant asserted the slave is free, he has implicitly placed the diyah on the 'aqilah and exonerated both the slave (as a free man) and the master (who has no liability after freeing). Thus, he cannot claim from them.
This is a consequence of his own admission.
4
Recognize the evidentiary bar
The claim against the 'aqilah requires proof (e.g., evidence of manumission). Without such proof, the 'aqilah are not obligated. Therefore, the claimant has no recourse against anyone.
The ruling ends with 'except that he is not believed against the 'aqilah without proof'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وإذا كان العبد لرجل زعم رجل أن مولاه أعتقه فقتل العبد وليا لذلك الرجل خطأ فلاشيء له لأنه لما زعم أن مولاه اعتقه فقد ادعى الدية على العاقلة وأبرأ العبد والمولى إلا أنه لا يصدق على العاقلة من غير حجة
He said: When a slave belongs to a man, and another man claims that his master freed him, then the slave accidentally kills the wali (guardian) of that man. Then nothing is due to him (the claimant). Because when he claimed that the master freed him, he asserted the diyah (blood money) is on the 'aqilah (tribal guarantors) and exonerated the slave and the master, except that he is not believed against the 'aqilah without proof.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This is a concise ruling. The scenario: A slave belongs to one man (the master). A third person claims that the master freed the slave. Then that slave accidentally kills the wali (family member) of that third person. The ruling: 'Nothing is due to him' — meaning the claimant has no claim for diyah from the slave or the master. The reasoning: by claiming the slave is free, the claimant has effectively admitted that if the slave were to kill anyone, the diyah would be on the 'aqilah (tribal guarantors) of the slave (since a free man's accidental killing liability falls on his 'aqilah), and he exonerates the slave (who as a free man would not be liable) and the master (who has no liability after freeing). However, this claim of freedom is not accepted against the actual 'aqilah unless the claimant provides proof. So practically, the claimant has no remedy: he cannot claim from the master or slave, and his claim against the 'aqilah is unsubstantiated without evidence.

Key Terms
عاقلة ('aqilah): The group of paternal male relatives or tribal supporters who pay diyah for accidental killings.
دية (diyah): Blood money paid for accidental killing or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a man claims a slave is freed, and then the slave accidentally kills the man's wali, why does the man receive nothing?
A) Because his claim exonerates the slave and master, and his claim against the 'aqilah is unproven.
B) Because the slave is still a slave, so the master is liable.
C) Because accidental killing has no diyah.
D) Because the wali is not a valid heir.

Key Takeaway
A claimant's own legal assertion can bar his recovery if it contradicts his current claim.

Sale of Child of Indebted Ma'dhuna Slave vs. Jinayah
Ruling with proof explaining sarayah of legal attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the child of an indebted ma'dhuna slave is sold with her for debt but not for jinayah, distinguishing between wasf hukmi and wasf haqiqi.

Core Idea
Debt is a legal attribute that spreads to the child; jinayah requires physical handover which does not spread.

This passage from Imam Muhammad's ruling in al-Hidayah presents a nuanced distinction between debt and criminal liability in the context of a ma'dhuna slave woman. You will learn how the Hanafi school uses the concept of sarayah (spreading of legal rulings) to determine that the child is sold with the mother for debt but not for her crime.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios and their rulings
The passage gives two cases: (1) debt exceeding the slave's value – the child is sold with her; (2) jinayah (crime) – the child is not surrendered. Note the opposite outcomes.
2
Understand the analogy to pawned slave
The debt case is likened to a pawned slave (marhunah) whose child is also pawned. The underlying illa (effective cause) is that debt is a legal attribute (wasf hukmi) attached to the neck.
Qiyas: child of pawned slave is sold with mother for the mortgage debt.
3
Distinguish legal attribute from real act
Legal attributes (awsaf hukmiyya) exist in the law's consideration and can spread (sarayah) to connected entities. Real acts (awsaf haqiqiyya) like physical handover for jinayah do not spread.
The key principle: sarayah occurs only in legal attributes, not real ones.
4
Apply the principle to the two cases
For debt: the legal attribute of debt spreads to the child, so the child is surrendered. For jinayah: the obligation to hand over the slave is a real act falling on the master, not a legal attribute on the slave; therefore it does not spread.
The master is liable for the jinayah, not the slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وإذا استدانت الأمة المأذون لها أكثر من قيمتها ثم ولدت فإنه يباع الولد معها في الدين وإن جنت جناية لم يدفع الولد معها والفرق أن الدين وصف حكمى فيها واجب في ذمتها متعلق برقبتها استيفاء. فيسرى إلى الولد كولد المرهونة بخلاف الجناية لأن وجوب الدفع فى ذمة المولى لا في ذمتها وإنما يلاقيها أثر الفعل الحقيقى وهو الدفع والسراية فى الأوصاف الشرعية دون الأوصاف الحقيقية
He said: When a female slave who has been given permission to trade incurs a debt exceeding her value, then she gives birth, the child is sold with her for the debt. If she commits a crime (jinayah), the child is not surrendered with her. The difference is that debt is a legal attribute (wasf hukmi) that is obligatory on her, attached to her neck for collection. So it spreads to the child, like the child of a pawned slave. In contrast to jinayah, because the obligation to hand over (the slave) is on the master (mawla), not on her. She is only met by the effect of the real act (al-fa'l al-haqiqi), which is the handing over. And the spreading (sarayah) occurs in legal attributes (al-awsaf al-shar'iyyah), not in real attributes.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Imam Muhammad explaining the difference between debt and jinayah regarding the child of a ma'dhuna slave woman. The slave woman had permission to trade and incurred a debt more than her value, then gave birth. For debt, both mother and child are sold to satisfy the debt. But if she committed a crime (jinayah) and then gave birth, the child is not surrendered with her. The reason: debt is a legal attribute attached to the slave's neck, like a pawned item, so it extends to the child. However, committing a crime obligates the master to surrender the slave (real act), not a legal attribute, and real acts do not spread to the child. The child remains with the master.

Key Terms
الأمة المأذون لها (al-ummah al-ma'dhun laha): A female slave whom the master has authorized to engage in trade (ma'dhuna).
وصف حكمى (wasf hukmi): A legal attribute that exists in the law's consideration, not physically. Here it refers to the debt being a right attached to the slave's neck.
سراية (sarayah): The extension of a legal ruling from one entity to another when they are connected. Here, the ruling of debt spreads from the mother to her child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the child of an indebted ma'dhuna slave sold with her, but not if she commits a jinayah?
A) Because debt is a legal attribute that spreads to the child, while jinayah requires actual delivery of the slave.
B) Because debt is always greater than the slave's value.
C) Because jinayah is a sin, so the child should not be involved.
D) Because the child is not responsible for the debt or the crime.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is that debt is a legal attribute (wasf hukmi) which spreads (yasri) to the child, while the obligation to surrender for jinayah is a real act (wasf haqiqi) that does not spread.

Claimant of Slave's Freedom Gets No Compensation After Murder
Ruling: claimant's unproven assertion bars compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the reason why a claimant who asserts a slave's freedom cannot subsequently claim diyah from the 'aqilah, and apply the principle that one's own claim can discharge others.

Core Idea
A claimant cannot benefit from inconsistent positions: claiming freedom discharges liability, and unproven claims against the 'aqilah fail.

In this passage, Imam Muhammad presents a case where a man claims a slave is free, but then the slave kills the man's relative. The ruling is clear: he receives no compensation. The reasoning involves two key principles: the claimant's own statement discharges the slave and master, and his unsubstantiated claim against the 'aqilah is not accepted. Let's unpack this ruling and its underlying legal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the ruling
Read the passage carefully. Identify the parties: a slave, his master, a claimant, and the claimant's wali who is killed. The claimant asserts the slave is free. The ruling is that the claimant gets nothing (no diyah).
The ruling is not about the slave's guilt but about the claimant's entitlement.
2
Analyze the first reason: discharge of slave and master
The claimant's claim that the slave is free implies that the slave is no longer property and the master has no liability. By his own admission, he has 'freed' the slave and absolved the master. He cannot later hold them responsible for the killing.
This is based on the legal principle that a person's statement can affect their own rights.
3
Analyze the second reason: failure of claim against the 'aqilah
The claimant tries to claim diyah from the 'aqilah (the victim's tribe), but his claim is based solely on his own assertion that the slave was free. Since he has no proof, the 'aqilah is not obligated to pay. His claim is rejected.
The 'aqilah is only liable for a free victim; the claimant's unproven assertion does not establish the victim's status.
4
Understand the istihsan basis
The ruling is built on istihsan (juristic preference): it would be unjust to allow the claimant to simultaneously benefit from his claim (avoiding liability of slave/master) and impose liability on others without proof.
This prevents contradictory positions and protects the 'aqilah from unfounded claims.
5
Apply the principle to similar cases
Consider other scenarios where a person's own statement affects rights. For example, if a man says 'I am a freed slave' and then claims inheritance from his former master, his statement may block that claim.
The key is that a claim that benefits one party cannot later be used to harm another without evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وإذا كان العبد لرجل زعم رجل أن مولاه أعتقه فقتل العبد وليا لذلك الرجل خطأ فلاشيء له لأنه لما زعم أن مولاه اعتقه فقد ادعى الدية على العاقلة وأبرأ العبد والمولى إلا أنه لا يصدق على العاقلة من غير حجة
He said: When a slave belongs to a man, and a man claims that his master freed him, then the slave kills a wali (guardian) of that man by mistake. He gets nothing because when he claimed that his master freed him, he claimed the diyah on the 'aqilah (tribe) and discharged the slave and the master, except that he is not believed against the 'aqilah without proof.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Imam Muhammad concerning a scenario: a man (Zayd) claims that a slave (Sajid) belonging to Khalid has been freed by Khalid. Then the slave mistakenly kills Zayd's wali (relative). The ruling: Zayd gets nothing (no diyah). Why? Because by claiming the slave is free, Zayd himself has stated that the slave is no longer a slave and thus the master is not liable. He effectively demanded diyah from the 'aqilah (the tribe responsible for paying diyah for a free person's accidental killing). However, since Zayd is merely making a claim without evidence, his assertion against the 'aqilah is not accepted. Therefore, he cannot collect from anyone: not from the slave (since he claimed him free), not from the master (since he claimed the master freed him), and not from the 'aqilah (since his claim is unsubstantiated).

Key Terms
العاقلة (al-'āqilah): The group of relatives (tribe) who are collectively responsible for paying diyah (blood money) in cases of accidental killing or injury.
ولي (walī): Here it means the guardian or relative of the claimant who was killed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because by claiming the slave was freed, he discharged both slave and master, and his claim against the aqilah is unproven.
B) Because the slave is not responsible for the killing.
C) Because the master is immune from liability.
D) Because the 'aqilah is not liable for a slave's crime.

Key Takeaway
A claimant's own assertion can discharge others from liability, and an unproven claim does not impose liability on third parties.

Freed Slave's Claim of Slavery at Killing Time Accepted
Ruling on whether the freed slave's word is accepted about his status at time of killing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for accepting a freed slave's claim of being a slave at the time of killing and explain the analogy (qiyas) with claims of minority or insanity.

Core Idea
A freed slave's word is accepted because he denies liability by referencing a known prior state of slavery.

In criminal liability cases, the burden of proof often falls on the claimant. But what happens when the defendant points to a known fact that would shift liability away from him? This lesson from Al-Hidayah explores a scenario where a freed slave claims he was still a slave when he committed a mistaken killing. The ruling turns on procedural fairness and the principle that a known state (slavery) negates personal liability. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting claims
One party (the freed slave) claims he was a slave at the time of the killing; the victim's relative claims he was free. Determine who bears the burden of proof.
The freed slave is the defendant (munkir li al-daman).
2
Apply the principle of 'munkir li al-daman'
In Hanafi law, the denier's statement is accepted with an oath. Here the freed slave denies personal liability for diyah, so his word is prima facie preferred.
But there must be something supporting his denial.
3
Identify the supporting state (ḥāl ma‘hūda)
The freed slave's previous slavery was a well-known state. This known state is inconsistent with him being personally liable (since a slave's crime obligates the master).
The criterion is that the state is 'ma‘hūda' – publicly known before emancipation.
4
Understand the analogy (qiyas) with minority/insanity cases
If a mature sane person claims he divorced his wife while a minor or insane, his word is accepted because the known state of incapacity negates the legal effect. Similarly, the known state of slavery negates personal liability for the slain.
The familiar cases serve as the aṣl (original) for the qiyas.
5
Derive the ruling and its limit
The freed slave's word is accepted, so he is not personally liable. Liability falls on the master (if still alive) or on the ‘āqila if applicable. This holds only when the slavery was known (ma‘rūf).
If his slavery was not known, different rules apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وإذا أعتق العبد فقال لرجل قتلت أخالك خطأ وأنا عبد وقال الآخر قتلته وأنت حر فالقول قول العبد لأنه منكر للضمان لما أنه أسنده إلى حالة معهودة منافية للضمان إذ الكلام فيما إذا عرف رقه والوجوب في جناية العبد على المولى دفعا أو فداء وصار كما إذا قال البالغ العاقل طلقت امرأتي وأنا صبى أو بعت دارى وأنا صبى أو قال طلقت امرأتـي وأنـا مـجـنـون وقـد كـان جـنـونـه مـعـروفـا كان القول قوله لما ذكرنا
He said: When a slave is freed, and then he says to a man, 'I killed your brother by mistake, and I was a slave (at that time),' and the other man says, 'You killed him while you were free,' then the word is the word of the slave (i.e., his statement is accepted). Because he is denying liability (munkir li al-daman), as he attributed (the act) to a known state (hala ma'huda) that is inconsistent with liability. The discussion is in a case where his slavery is known, and the obligation for the crime of a slave is on the master, either by handing over (the slave) or by ransom (fida). And it becomes like when a mature, sane person says, 'I divorced my wife while I was a minor,' or 'I sold my house while I was a minor,' or he says, 'I divorced my wife while I was insane,' and his insanity was known, then his word is accepted, because of what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Imam Muhammad about a freed slave (Sajid) who tells the victim's brother (Zayd) that he killed Zayd's brother (Bakr) by mistake while he (Sajid) was still a slave. Zayd disputes this, claiming Sajid was free at the time of killing. The ruling: Sajid's statement is accepted (his word is taken). Why? Because Sajid is denying liability for diyah (he claims liability was on his former master). He attributes the act to a known state (slavery), which is inconsistent with him being personally liable. When Sajid's slavery was widely known (ma'ruf), his claim that he was then a slave is consistent with that known state. Moreover, the liability for a slave's crime falls on the master (to surrender or ransom). By analogy, if a mature sane person says he divorced his wife while he was a minor (and his minority is known), his word is accepted because he denies the legal effect of divorce. Similarly, if he claims he divorced while insane, and his insanity is known, his word is accepted. Thus, the freed slave's word is taken to avoid imposing liability upon him based on a mere counter-claim.

Key Terms
حالة معهودة (hāl ma’hūdah): A well-known state or condition that is publicly known and acknowledged (here: the slavery of the freed slave was known before his emancipation).
منكر للضمان (munkir li al-ḍamān): One who denies liability or guarantee. Here the freed slave denies personal liability for the diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because he denies liability and attributes the act to a known state (slavery) that negates personal liability.
B) Because he is the defendant and defendants always have the benefit of the doubt.
C) Because the victim's relative was not present at the killing.
D) Because the slave had already been freed before the trial.

Key Takeaway
A freed slave's claim of being a slave at the time of a mistaken killing is accepted because he denies liability based on a known prior state, similar to claims of minority or insanity.

Credibility of a Person Claiming Minority or Insanity
Supporting precedents for accepting the denier's claim of incapacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on accepting the word of a person who denies liability by attributing an action to a well-known state of minority or insanity, and apply the principle to analogous cases.

Core Idea
Denier's word is accepted if he attributes the act to a known state of incapacity.

The core legal problem: when someone claims that an injurious act occurred during a period of legal incapacity (minority or insanity), should his word be accepted? The Hanafi school answers affirmatively, provided that the claimed state was well-known. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its proof through three illustrative examples, revealing the underlying qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The issue is whether a person's claim that he was a minor or insane at the time of an act (e.g., divorce, sale, killing) is to be accepted, thereby negating liability.
The person is denying liability (munkir al-daman).
2
State the Hanafi rule
If the claimed state (minority or insanity) is well-known (halat ma'hudah), the person's word is accepted without further proof. He is not liable.
The burden of proof is on the claimant, not the denier.
3
Examine the supporting examples
Three cases: (1) divorce as a child, (2) sale as a child, (3) divorce while insane when insanity was known. In each, the person's word is accepted because he is denying liability and the state is notorious.
The examples show the breadth of the principle beyond diyat.
4
Extract the common illa
The effective cause (illa) is that the denier attributes the act to a well-known state of legal incapacity, which makes the denial credible.
The state must be known (ma'hudah), not merely asserted.
5
Apply to the original case
A slave whose enslavement is well-known claims he was a minor when he committed the injurious act. Since he is a munkir al-daman and his state (minority) is well-known (by inference from his known enslavement), his word is accepted, and the master bears liability.
This extends the rule from persons to slaves with known status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تو بتایا کہ کیونکہ وقتل کی اسناد ایسی حالت کی جانب کر رہا ہے جس میں اس کے اوپر ضمان واجب نہیں کیا جا سکتا ، حالت معہودہ سے مراد یہی ہے کہ اس غلام کی رقیت معروف ومشہور ہو کیونکہ اس کا قول معتبر ہونے کی صورت میں آقا پر مضمان ہوگا ، خواہ اس غلام کو حوالہ کرتے یا پھر فدیہ ادا کرے۔

قول معتبر ہوگا۔
He explained that because the attribution of the killing is to a state in which liability cannot be imposed upon him. The habitual state intended is that the slave's enslavement is well-known, because if his word were accepted, the master would be held liable, whether by handing over the slave or paying ransom. In support of this, he presented three examples: (1) a sane adult says: 'When I was a child, I divorced my wife' - his word is accepted. (2) I sold my house when I was a child - his word is accepted. (3) I divorced my wife when I was insane, and that incident of insanity is well-known - his word is accepted. In all these cases, his word is accepted because he is the denier of liability. Similarly, in the previous case, the denier, i.e., the slave, his word is accepted.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a ruling concerning a slave who claims he was a minor when he committed an injurious act against his master. The author explains the underlying principle: if the slave's enslavement was well-known, his claim of being a minor at the time of the act is accepted because he is denying liability (daman). The master would be liable only if the slave's word were not accepted. To support this, three analogous cases are presented: (1) a person claims he was a child when he divorced his wife – his word is accepted because he denies the legal effect of divorce; (2) a person claims he was a child when he sold his house – his word is accepted because he denies the validity of the sale; (3) a person claims he was insane at the time of divorce, and his insanity was well-known – his word is accepted. In all these, the person is the denier (munkir) of liability, and the principle is that the denier's word is accepted. The final sentence applies this to the slave in the prior case, establishing the rule in the chapter of diyat.

Key Terms
منکر ضمان (munkir daman): The person who denies being liable for compensation or punishment.
حالت معہودہ (halat ma'hudah): A well-known and established state or condition of a person (e.g., being a slave or insane).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a person's claim of minority or insanity at the time of an act is accepted without proof if that state is well-known, because he is the denier of liability.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Credibility of Master vs Freed Slave-Girl in Injury Claims
Intra-Hanafi dispute about credibility of master vs freed slave-girl in injury claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on credibility in disputes between a master and his freed slave-girl, and evaluate Imam Muhammad's objection using the principle of admission of cause of liability.

Core Idea
A master who admits taking property cannot later claim ownership to avoid liability – his word is not accepted, except for intercourse and earnings by istihsan.

This lesson presents a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute that tests the boundary between procedural justice (who speaks truth?) and substantive liability. When a freed slave-girl claims injuries and seizure of her property after emancipation, but her former master insists it all happened during ownership, the Hanafi masters split on whose word prevails. The two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) hold that the master, by admitting the act of taking, has conceded the cause of liability; his subsequent claim of exemption is thus suspect. Imam Muhammad, however, sees the master as the denier of liability because his story fits a habitual state (ownership) incompatible with liability. The dispute turns on a subtle qiyas (analogical reasoning) and the application of istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute and the two positions
Read the scenario: master frees slave-girl, then claims he cut her hand and took her belongings while she was his property. She says it happened after freedom. The two shaykhs hold that the slave-girl's word is accepted for all taken property except intercourse and earnings. Imam Muhammad holds that the master is liable only for property that still exists.
The exception for intercourse and earnings is based on istihsan because those acts are normally consented to during slavery.
2
Analyze the two shaykhs' reasoning: admission of cause of liability
The two shaykhs argue that the master, by saying 'I took her property,' has admitted to the act that creates liability (muqir bi sabab al-daman). His subsequent claim that it happened during slavery is an exemption (ibrāʼ) that he must prove. Since he admitted the cause, his word is not accepted; the slave-girl is the denier (munkira) of his ownership at the time.
This principle appears in the eye-gouging analogy: a person who admits gouging an eye cannot then claim that his own eye was sound to avoid liability.
3
Examine Imam Muhammad's view: master as the denier
Imam Muhammad counters that the master is actually the denier of liability (munkir al-daman) because he attributes the act to a time when she was his property – a habitual state incompatible with full liability. Therefore, the master's word should be accepted for all claims except property that still exists, where he has clearly admitted her possession.
He draws analogy with the master's word being accepted in intercourse and earnings, and with a slave's word in a similar earlier case.
4
Evaluate the two shaykhs' proof via qiyas
The two shaykhs present a formal qiyas: the original case (asl) is a person who admits gouging someone's right eye but claims his own eye was sound at the time (later gouged by another). The admittee's word is accepted. The new case (far') is the master who admits taking property but claims it was during slavery. The effective cause (illa) is admission of the liability-causing act followed by a claim of exemption. The ruling (hukm) is that the admitter's word is not accepted.
This is a qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) because the illa is implicit: admission of the act shifts the burden.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon position is that of the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Practically, the master must return all taken property except for value of intercourse and earnings. According to Imam Muhammad, he only returns property still in existence.
The difference is significant for consumed or destroyed property. Under the mufta bihi, the master owes compensation for everything taken (except the two exceptions).

ایک شخص نے کسی باندی کو آزاد کیا پھر اس سے کہا کہ میں نے تیرا ہاتھ کاٹا تھا حالانکہ تو میری باندی تھی اور باندی نے کہا کہ تو نے میرا ہاتھ کاٹا جب کہ میں حر تھی تو باندی کا قول معتبر ہے

قال ومن أعتق جارية ثم قال لها قطعت يدك وأنت أمتى وقالت قطعتها وأنا حرة فالقول قولها وكذلك كل ما أخذ منها إلا الـجـمـاع والغلة استحسانا وهذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف رحمهما الله

ترجمہ: ام محمد نے فرمایا اور جس نے کسی باندی کو آزاد کیا پھر اس سے کہا کہ میں نے تیرا ہاتھ کاٹا تھا حالانکہ تو میری باندی تھی اور باندی نے کہا کہ تونے میرا ہاتھ کاٹا تھا حالانکہ میں حر تھی تو باندی کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ اور ایسے ہی جملہ ان چیزوں میں جو باندی سے لی گئی ہوں علاوہ جماع اور کمائی کے استحسانا، اور یہ شیخین کے نزدیک ہے۔

تشریح: ساجدہ خالد کی باندی تھی خالد نے اس کو آزاد کر دیا اب خالد ساجدہ سے کہتا ہے کہ میں نے تیرا ہاتھ کاٹا تھا جب کہ تو میری باندی تھی اور ساجدہ کہتی ہے کہ نہیں بلکہ اس وقت ہاتھ کاٹا ہے جب کہ میں آزاد ہو چکی تھی تو یہاں باندی کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔ اور جماع اور کمائی کے علاوہ جو چیز بھی ساجدہ سے لی گئی ہو اس میں یہی حکم ہے کہ باندی کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ ہاں جماع میں اگر یہ اختلاف ہو جائے تو استحسانا آقا کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔ اور اگر یہی اختلاف کمائی کے اندر ہو جائے تو بھی آقا کا قول معتبر ہوگا اور یہ حکم استحسانا ہے اور حضرات حسین کا مذہب ہے۔

امام محمد کا نقطہ نظر:
وقال محمد لا يضمن إلا شيئا قائما بعينه يؤمر برده عليها لأنه منكر وجوب الضمان إسناد الفعل إلى حالة معهودة منافية له كما فى المسئلة الأولى وكما فى الوطى والغلة وفى الشيء القائم أقر بيدها حيث اعترف

ترجمہ: اور ام محمد نے فرمایا کہ ضامن نہ ہو گا آقا کسی چیز کا مگر اس کا جو بعینہ قائم ہو تو آقا کو حکم دیا جائے گا اس کے واپس کرنے کا اس پر (باندی پر) اس لئے کہ آقا و جوب ضمان کا منکر ہے آقا کے فعل کی اسناد کرنے کی وجہ سے ایسی حالت معہودہ (معروفہ) کی جانب جو ضمان کے منافی ہے۔ جیسے پہلے مسئلہ میں اور جیسے وطی اور کمائی میں اور اس چیز میں موجود ہے آقا نے باندی کے قبضہ کا اقرار کر لیا اس حیثیت سے کہ وہ باندی سے لینے کا اعتراف کر چکا ہے پھر اس نے دعوی کیا باندی کے مالک ہونے کا حالانکہ وہ منکرہ ہے اور قول منکر معتبر ہوا کرتا ہے اسی وجہ سے تو آقا کو اس کی جانب واپس کرنے کا حکم دیا جاتا ہے۔

تشریح: پہلے مسئلہ میں شیخین کے نزدیک جماع اور آمدنی کے علاوہ چیزوں میں باندی کا قول معتبر ہو گا جو شیخین کا مذہب ہے اور امام محمد کا قول یہ ہے کہ شئی موجود کے علاوہ بقیہ کسی اور چیز کے رد کا حکم نہ ہوگا، البتہ اگر بعینہ وہی چیز موجود ہے تو آقا کو حکم دیا جائے گا کہ یہ سامان اس کے حوالہ کر دو کیونکہ اس صورت میں آقا خود معترف ہے کہ میں نے یہ سامان باندی سے لیا ہے اور پھر آقا اس باندی کے مالک، ہونے کا بھی مدعی ہوا ہے اور باندی منکر ہے لہذا منکر کا قول معتبر ہو گا اور آقا کو واپس کا حکم دیا جائے گا۔

اور شئی موجود کے علاوہ میں امام محمد کے نزدیک آقا کا قول معتبر ہوگا کیونکہ اس صورت میں آقا منکر اور باندی مدعیہ ہے اور منکر کا قول معتبر ہوا کرتا ہے آقا کے منکر ہونے کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ اس نے فعل کی اسناد ایسی حالت کی جانب کی ہے جو معہود ہے اور منافی مضمان ہے کیونکہ وہ اس کی باندی تھی جیسے اسی بنیاد پر پہلے مسئلہ میں غلام کا قول معتبر ہوا تھا اس لئے کہ وہ منکر تھا اور جیسے وطی میں اور کمائی میں آقا کا قول معتبر ہوا کرتا ہے اسی طرح تمام صورتوں میں آقا کا قول معتبر ہونا چاہیے یہ امام محمد کی دلیل تھی، آگے شیخین کی دلیل آرہی ہے۔

شیخین کی دلیل:
ولهما أنه أقر بسبب الضمان ثم ادعى ما يبرئه فلايكون القول قوله كما إذا قال لغيره فقات عینک الیمنی وعينى اليمينى صحيحة ثم فقئت وقال المقرله لا بل فقأتها و عینک الیمنی مفقؤة فإن القول قول المقر له

ترجمہ: اور شیخین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ آقا نے ضمان کے سبب کا اقرار کر لیا پھر ایسی چیز کا دعوی کیا جو اس کو ضمان سے بجا کر دے تو اس کا قول معتبر نہ ہو گا جیسے کسی نے اپنے غیر سے کہا کہ میں نے تیری دائیں آنکھ پھوڑی تھی حالانکہ میری دائیں آنکھ ٹھیک تھی پھر پھوڑ دی گئی اور مقرلہ نے کہا نہیں بلکہ تو نے اس کو پھوڑا تھا حالانکہ تیری دائیں آنکھ پھوٹی ہوئی تھی پس مقرلہ کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔

تشریح: حضرات شیخین فرماتے ہیں کہ اولاً آقا نے یہ کہہ کر کہ میں نے یہ سامان اس باندی کا لیا ہے، سبب ضمان کا اقرار کر لیا ہے اور پھر یوں کہہ کر کہ میں نے تو جب لیا تھا جب کہ میری باندی تھی، ضمان سے برکت کا دعوی کرتا ہے اور ایسے شخص کا قول غیر معتبر ہوتا ہے۔ لہذا یہاں آقا کا قول غیر معتبر ہوگا۔ اس کی توضیح اس مثال سے ہو سکتی ہے کہ ایک شخص اقرار کرتا ہے کہ میں نے تیری دائیں آنکھ پھوڑی تھی لیکن اس وقت پھوڑی جب کہ میری آنکھ صحیح تھی اور میری آنکھ بعد میں پھوڑی گئی ہے اگر اس کی آنکھ پہلے سے پھوٹی ہوئی تھی تو مقر پر آنکھ کا ارش واجب ہوتا اور اگر صحیح ہوتی تو قصاص ہوتا مگر یہاں بعد میں وہ کسی اور نے پھوڑ دی تو محل قصاص نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے قصاص بھی ختم ہو گیا اور ارش بھی۔
A person freed a slave-girl and then said to her: 'I cut your hand while you were my slave,' and she said: 'You cut my hand while I was free.' The slave-girl's word is accepted. Ibn Muhammad said: 'And whoever frees a slave-girl then says to her: I cut your hand while you were my slave, and she says: You cut it while I was free, then her word is accepted, and similarly for everything taken from her except intercourse and earnings, by istihsan. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on them.' Translation: Ibn Muhammad said: And whoever frees a slave-girl then says to her: I cut your hand while you were my slave, and she says: You cut it while I was free, then her word is accepted. And similarly for everything taken from her except intercourse and earnings, by istihsan, and this is the view of the two shaykhs. Commentary: Sajidah was Khalid's slave-girl. Khalid freed her. Now Khalid says to Sajidah: I cut your hand while you were my slave, and Sajidah says: No, you cut it when I was already free. Here the slave-girl's word is accepted. And for anything taken from Sajidah besides intercourse and earnings, the same ruling applies: the slave-girl's word is accepted. Yes, if the dispute is about intercourse, then by istihsan the master's word is accepted. And if the dispute is about earnings, the master's word is also accepted, by istihsan, and this is the school of the two shaykhs. Imam Muhammad's view: He said: The master is not liable except for something that still exists in its original form; he will be ordered to return it to her, because he is denying the obligation of liability due to attributing the act to a habitual state incompatible with liability, like in the first case and like in intercourse and earnings. And in the case of the existing thing, he acknowledged that she had possession, in the sense that he confessed to taking it from her, then claimed ownership over her while she is denying it, and the denier's word is accepted. Therefore, he is ordered to return it to her. Commentary: In the first case, according to the two shaykhs, for things other than intercourse and earnings, the slave-girl's word is accepted. This is the school of the two shaykhs. Imam Muhammad's view is that only for the existing thing is an order of return given. However, if the thing still exists in its original form, then the master will be ordered to hand over that item to her, because in this case the master himself admits that he took this item from the slave-girl, and then he also claims ownership of her, and the slave-girl denies it. Therefore, the denier's word is accepted and the master is ordered to return it. And for things other than the existing item, according to Imam Muhammad, the master's word is accepted, because in that case the master is the denier and the slave-girl is the claimant, and the denier's word is accepted. The reason the master is the denier is that he attributed the act to a habitual state incompatible with liability, because she was his slave. Just as on this basis, in the first case the slave's word was accepted because he was the denier, and just as in intercourse and earnings the master's word is accepted, similarly in all cases the master's word should be accepted. This was Imam Muhammad's proof. Next comes the two shaykhs' proof. The two shaykhs' proof: And for them (they argue) that he admitted the cause of liability, then claimed something that would exempt him, so his word is not accepted, just as if someone said to another: 'I gouged out your right eye while my right eye was sound, and then it was gouged out,' and the admittee said: 'No, you gouged it out while your right eye was already gouged out,' then the admittee's word is accepted. Translation: And the two shaykhs' proof is that the master admitted the cause of liability, then claimed something that would exempt him from liability, so his word is not accepted, just as if someone said to another: 'I gouged out your right eye while my right eye was sound, then it was gouged out,' and the admittee said: 'No, you gouged it out while your right eye was already gouged,' then the admittee's word is accepted. Commentary: The two shaykhs state that firstly the master, by saying 'I took this item from this slave-girl,' admitted the cause of liability, and then by saying 'I took it when she was my slave,' he claimed exemption from liability. The word of such a person is not accepted. Therefore, here the master's word is not accepted. This can be clarified by the example: a person admits: 'I gouged out your right eye, but at the time of gouging my eye was sound and my eye was later gouged out.' If his eye had been already gouged out, then arsh (compensation) would be wajib on the admitter, and if it had been sound, then qisas would apply. But here, since someone else later gouged it out, the qisas is nullified and also the arsh...
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents a complete intra-Hanafi dispute between the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Imam Muhammad regarding a case where a master frees his slave-girl and then claims he cut her hand while she was his slave, while she claims it happened after freedom. The passage begins by stating the position of the two shaykhs: the slave-girl's word is accepted for all property claims except for intercourse (where the master's word is accepted because she would not have consented if free) and earnings (also credited to the master by istihsan). The underlying principle is that the slave-girl is the denier (munkira) of the master's ownership at the time of the act, and the denier's word is accepted. Imam Muhammad disagrees, limiting the master's liability only to property that still exists in its original form (shay' qa'im bi 'aynihi). He argues that in all other cases, the master is the denier of liability because he attributes the act to a time when she was his property, which is a habitual state incompatible with liability. To him, the slave-girl is the claimant and must prove her case. The two shaykhs respond with an analogy: if a person admits to gouging out someone's eye but claims his own eye was sound at the time (later gouged), his word is not accepted because he first admitted the cause of liability (the act of gouging) and then claimed an exemption (soundness of his eye). Similarly, the master admits to taking the property (admission of cause of liability) and then claims it was while she was his slave (exemption). Therefore, his word is not accepted, and the slave-girl's word prevails. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of the two shaykhs, as it is the madhhab of the majority and the matn's ruling.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference used to depart from strict qiyas in favor of a ruling that better serves justice or ease. Here, it is applied to accept the master's word in cases of intercourse and earnings despite the general rule.
مقر بسبب الضمان (muqir bi sabab al-daman): One who admits to the cause that would entail liability (e.g., admitting to taking property). In the analysis, the master becomes a muqir because he confesses to taking the slave-girl's property.
منكر (munkir): The person who denies the claim against him. In the dispute, the slave-girl is considered the munkira (denier) of the master's ownership at the time of the act, so her word is accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position of the two shaykhs, which of the following is an exception where the master's word is accepted over the slave-girl's word?
A) All property claims.
B) Only property that still exists.
C) Intercourse and earnings.
D) Property that has been consumed.

Key Takeaway
A master who admits taking property triggers liability; his word is not accepted to escape that liability, except for intercourse and earnings by istihsan.

Credibility of Master vs Slave-Girl in Liability Claims
Explaining the rule for accepting the slave-girl's statement over the master's in injury and property claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the slave-girl's statement is accepted over the master's in liability disputes and identify the two exceptions (wati and ghalla) with their rationale.

Core Idea
The slave-girl's statement prevails except in intercourse and income, where liability never arises.

This passage presents a key Hanafi ruling on disputes between a master and his slave-girl over liability for injury or property. The Shaikhan (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) provide a nuanced reasoning using analogies and exceptions. You will learn to apply this rule and understand why the master's statement is accepted only in two specific cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disputed Scenario
The passage concerns a disagreement between a master (muqirr) and his slave-girl (muqarrun lahu) about when an action occurred. The master claims he acted when no liability existed (e.g., she was already injured), while the slave-girl claims it happened when liability was possible (e.g., she was healthy or indebted).
The general rule: the slave-girl's statement is accepted.
2
Understand the General Rule
The base text states that the confessor's (master's) claim is not accepted; the confessee's (slave-girl's) claim is accepted. This is because ownership does not automatically negate liability.
3
Analyze the Shaikhan's Proof
Shaikhan argue that liability is possible even though the victim is a slave, by analogy: a master who cuts his indebted slave-girl's hand is liable; similarly, a Muslim who takes property from a harbi musta'min is liable. Thus, the master's attribution to a non-liable state is not accepted for general acts.
The proof uses qiyas (analogy) to establish that liability is not incompatible with ownership.
4
Identify the Two Exceptions
The exceptions are intercourse (wati) and income (ghalla). In these, the master is never liable even if the slave-girl is indebted, so his statement attributing the act to a non-liable state is accepted.
The reasoning is that these acts are inherently non-liable.
5
Apply the Rule to New Cases
When presented with a dispute, first determine whether the act is one of the two exceptions. If not, accept the slave-girl's statement. If it is one of the exceptions, accept the master's statement.
This distinction is crucial for correct fatwa.

بہر حال مقر کہتا ہے کہ میں نے تیری آنکھ جب پھوڑی تھی جب کہ میری آنکھ ٹھیک تھی اور مقرلہ کہتا ہے کہ جب پھوڑی جب کہ تیری آنکھ پھوٹی ہوئی تھی تو یہاں مقر کا قول معتبر نہیں بلکہ مقرلہ کا قول معتبر ہوگا ، لہذا اسی طرح مسئلہ مذکورہ میں بھی مقررہ یعنی باندی کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔

شیخین کا نقطہ نظر
وَهَذَا لِأَنَّهُ مَا أَسْنَدَ إِلَى حَالَةٍ مُنَافِيَةٍ لِلضَّمَانِ لِأَنَّهُ يَضْمَنُ يَدَهَا لَوْ قَطَعَهَا وَهِيَ مَدْيُونَةٌ وَكَذَا يَضْمَنُ مَالَ الْحَرْبِيِّ إِذَا أَخَذَهُ وَهُوَ مُسْتَأْمِنٌ بِخِلَافِ الْوَطْءِ وَالْغَلَّةِ لِأَنَّ وَطْءَ الْمَوْلَى أَمَتَهُ الْمَدْيُونَةَ لَا يُوجِبُ الْعَقْرَ وَكَذَا أَخْذُهُ مِنْ غَلَّتِهَا وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مَدْيُونَةً لَا يُوجِبُ الضَّمَانَ عَلَيْهِ فَحَصَلَ الْإِسْنَادُ إِلَى حَالَةٍ مَعْهُودَةٍ مُنَافِيَةٍ لِلضَّمَانِ۔

ترجمہ: اور یہ اس لئے کہ آقا نے فعل کی اسناد ایسی حالت کی جانب نہیں کی جو ضمان کے منافی ہو اس لئے کہ آقا باندی کے ہاتھ کا ضامن ہوتا ہے اگر اس کا ہاتھ کاٹا ہو حالانکہ وہ مدیونہ ہو اور ایسے ہی مسلمان حربی کے مال کا ضامن ہوتا ہے جب کہ مسلمان نے اس کو لیا ہو اور وہ مسلمان مستامن ہو بخلاف وطی اور کمائی کے اس لئے کہ آقا کا وطی کرنا اپنی مدیونہ باندی سے عقر کو واجب نہیں کرتا اور ایسے ہی آقا کا لینا اس کی کمائی سے اگر چہ وہ مدیون ہو آقا پر ضمان کو واجب نہیں کرتا تو فعل کی اسناد ایسی حالت کی جانب حاصل ہوئی جو معروف ہے ضمان کے منافی ہے۔

تشریح: شیخین فرماتے ہیں کہ ہم نے آقا کے لینے کے اقرار کو اس لئے ضمان کا سبب قرار دیا کہ یہ ممکن ہے کہ باندی اور غلام ہوتے ہوئے بھی آقا پر اس جرم کا ضمان واجب ہو سکتا ہے یعنی وجوب ضمان اور مملوک ہونے میں کوئی منافات نہیں ہے کیونکہ اگر آقا نے اپنی باندی کا ہاتھ کاٹ لیا اور اس پر کسی کا قرض ہے تو باندی ہونے کے باوجود آقا پر ضمان واجب ہوگا۔ اسی طرح اگر ایک مسلمان امان لے کر دار الحرب میں آیا اور وہاں کسی کافر کا مال لیا اور وہ حربی بھی مسلمان ہو کر دار السلام میں آ گیا اب مسلمان اقرار کرتا ہے کہ میں نے تیرا مال جب لیا تھا جب کہ تو کافر تھا اور دوسرا کہتا ہے کہ جب لیا تھا جب کہ میں مسلمان ہو چکا تھا، تو یہاں مقرلہ کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔ اور مسلمان پر ضمان واجب ہوگا، کیونکہ جس طرح فعل کی اسناد پہلے مسئلہ میں ایسی حالت کی جانب نہیں ہے جو منافی ضمان ہو (کما مر) یہاں بھی فعل کی اسناد ایسی حالت کی جانب نہیں جو منافی ضمان ہو کیونکہ فی الجملہ حربی کا مال لینا بھی قابل ضمان ہے۔ لہذا اگر کوئی حربی امان لیکر دار الاسلام میں آئے اور کوئی مسلمان یا ذمی اس کا مال لے لے تو ضمان واجب ہوگا۔

سوال: تو پھر شیخین نے وطی اور کمائی کی صورت میں کیوں باندی کا قول معتبر نہیں مانتا بلکہ آقا کا قول معتبر مان لیا ہے؟ جواب: اس لئے کہ اگر آقا اپنی مدیونہ باندی سے وطی کرلے تو آقا پر کوئی ضمان یا کوئی عقر واجب نہیں ہے اسی طرح اگر آقا اپنی مدیونہ باندی کی کمائی میں سے کچھ لے لے تو آقا پر کوئی ضمان واجب نہیں ہے۔ الہذا ان دونوں صورتوں میں جب آقا یوں کہے کہ میں نے وطی کی تھی یا کمائی کی تھی مگر اس وقت جب کہ تو میری باندی تھی تو آقا اپنے فعل کی اسناد اس حالت معہودہ کی جانب کر رہا ہے جو منافی ضمان ہے اور ان دونوں کے علاوہ میں منافات ثابت نہ ہوسکی، اس لئے تمام صورتوں میں باندی کا قول معتبر ہوا اور فقط ان دو صورتوں میں آقا کا قول معتبر ہوا ہے۔
In any case, the confessor says: I poked out your eye when my eye was healthy, and the confessee says: you poked it when my eye was already poked out. Here the confessor's statement is not accepted; rather the confessee's statement will be accepted. Therefore, similarly in the mentioned issue, the confessee, i.e., the slave-girl's statement will be accepted.

Shaikhan's perspective: And that is because he did not attribute (the action) to a state incompatible with liability, for he is liable for her hand if he cuts it while she is indebted, and likewise he is liable for the property of a harbi if he takes it while he (the harbi) is a musta'min, contrary to intercourse (wati) and income (ghalla), because the master's intercourse with his indebted slave-girl does not necessitate 'aqr, and likewise his taking from her income, even if she is indebted, does not necessitate liability upon him. Thus the attribution is to a known state that is incompatible with liability.

Translation: And this is because the master did not attribute the action to a state that is contrary to liability, because the master is liable for the hand of his slave-girl if he cuts it while she is indebted, and similarly he is liable for the property of the harbi if he takes it while the harbi is a musta'min, unlike intercourse and income, because the master's intercourse with his indebted slave-girl does not obligate 'aqr, and similarly his taking from her income, even if she is indebted, does not obligate liability upon him. So the attribution is to a state that is known to be contrary to liability.

Commentary: Shaikhan state: We made the master's confession of taking a cause for liability because it is possible that even though the slave-girl is a slave, liability for the crime can be imposed on the master, i.e., there is no contradiction between the obligation of liability and being a slave. For if the master cuts off the hand of his slave-girl and she has a debt, he is liable despite her being a slave. Similarly, if a Muslim enters Dar al-Harb with aman and takes the property of a harbi, and then the harbi becomes Muslim and comes to Dar al-Islam, and the Muslim confesses, 'I took your property when you were a disbeliever,' while the other says, 'You took it when I had already become Muslim,' then the confessee's statement is accepted, and the Muslim is liable, because just as in the earlier issue the attribution of the action was not to a state contrary to liability, here too the attribution is not to a state contrary to liability, since taking the property of a harbi is, in principle, subject to liability. Therefore, if a harbi comes to Dar al-Islam with aman and a Muslim or dhimmi takes his property, liability is obligatory.

Question: Then why in the case of intercourse and income did Shaikhan not accept the slave-girl's statement but instead accepted the master's statement? Answer: Because if the master has intercourse with his indebted slave-girl, no liability or 'aqr is obligatory upon him. Similarly, if the master takes something from the income of his indebted slave-girl, no liability is obligatory upon him. Hence, in these two cases, when the master says, 'I had intercourse or I took income, but at a time when you were my slave-girl,' the master is attributing his action to a state that is incompatible with liability. In cases other than these two, incompatibility could not be established. Therefore, in all other cases the slave-girl's statement is accepted, and only in these two cases is the master's statement accepted.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school regarding whose statement is accepted when a master and his slave-girl disagree about the timing of an action that could impose liability. The central rule is that the slave-girl's statement is normally accepted over the master's, except in two specific cases: intercourse (wati) with the slave-girl and taking her income (ghalla). The text first presents the scenario: if the master says he injured the slave-girl when he was healthy, and she says she was already injured, the slave-girl's statement is accepted. The same applies to the master's taking property or causing injury. Then Shaikhan (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) provide the reasoning: the master's claim that the action occurred while the slave-girl was his property (and thus not liable) is not accepted because liability can still exist in that state. For example, if the master cuts his slave-girl's hand while she is indebted, he is liable. Similarly, a Muslim who takes property from a harbi musta'min is liable. However, in the cases of wati and ghalla, the master is not liable even if the slave-girl is indebted, because these actions are inherently non-liable. Thus, when the master attributes his action to that state (i.e., to the time when she was his slave-girl), his statement is accepted. The commentary explains that the master's confession is considered a cause for liability because liability is not negated by the master's ownership—it is only negated for specific acts. The question-answer clarifies the exception: since wati and ghalla do not incur liability, the master's attribution to that state is credible, so his statement is accepted. In all other cases, the slave-girl's statement prevails.

Key Terms
مقر (muqirr): The party making the confession (here the master)
مقرلہ (muqarrun lahu): The party in whose favor the confession is made (here the slave-girl)
مدیونہ (madyunah): A female slave who is in debt to others
عقر (aqr): The compensation (dower) for sexual intercourse with a slave-girl
غلتہ (ghalla): Income or profit generated by the slave-girl (e.g., earnings from trade)
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim given safe passage (aman) into Muslim territory

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which two cases does the master's statement take precedence over the slave-girl's?
A) Injury and taking property
B) Intercourse (wati) and income (ghalla)
C) Cutting the hand and taking the slave's produce
D) Confession of debt and injury

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts the slave-girl's statement over the master's in liability disputes, except for intercourse and income, where the master's statement is accepted because those acts never incur liability.
